<script type="application/ld+json">{"@context":"https:\/\/schema.org","@type":"BlogPosting","headline":"Analyzing the Various Layers of AgentTesla\u2019s Packing","name":"Analyzing the Various Layers of AgentTesla\u2019s Packing","description":"Palo Alto Networks Unit 42 analyzes the layers of AgentTesla's Packing.","url":"https:\/\/unit42.paloaltonetworks.com\/unit42-analyzing-various-layers-agentteslas-packing\/","mainEntityOfPage":"https:\/\/unit42.paloaltonetworks.com\/unit42-analyzing-various-layers-agentteslas-packing\/","datePublished":"September 25, 2017","articleBody":"AgentTesla is a fairly popular key logger built using the Microsoft .NET Framework and has shown a substantial rise in usage over the past few months.\n\n\n\n&nbsp;\n\nIt offers all of the standard features of a keylogger but goes beyond the typical confines of this type of software. One particular feature of interest is the custom packer it uses to hide the primary AgentTesla binary. Packers allow for a binary to essentially be wrapped in another binary to mask the original one from detection.\n\nThere are a number of excellent blogs out there covering AgentTesla\u2019s functionality and it\u2019s various obfuscations, but having I recently unpacked a sample and wanted to focus on this particular function and provide some helpful tools to aide in unpacking it.\n\nFor this analysis, I\u2019ll be using a PE32 version AgentTesla file seen in the wild on August 29th with hash \u201cca29bd44fc1c4ec031eadf89fb2894bbe646bc0cafb6242a7631f7404ef7d15c\u201d. You\u2019ll find AgentTesla delivered commonly via phishing documents that usually contain VBA macros to download and run a file \u2013 like the one in question.\n\nAs it\u2019s a commercial product, you\u2019ll find a lot of variety in the initial carrier files that deliver the AgentTesla binary; however, at some point you\u2019ll find yourself with a PE.\nThus, begins the journey\u2026\nI suppose the first layer of obfuscation really begins with the file itself, called \u201cone.jpeg.png.exe\u201d and an icon of a JPG trying to create an illusion of legitimacy.\n\n\n\nThis is a common technique to fool people and they\u2019ve taken it one step further by opening an image when you execute the binary.\n\n\n\nThe first executable is a .NET application, which is no surprise since AgentTesla is very well known for being a .NET key logger. To analyze .NET applications, I prefer to use the application dnSpy and, once loading up this sample, we can see there is only one namespace of interest with a handful of functions and a byte array.\n\n\n\nThe Japanese kanji stands out at first glance but I believe this is less about language and more about being a form of obfuscation \u2013 I\u2019ll explain why shortly.\n\nLooking at the Main() function shows a pattern of multiple calls to two other functions.\n\n\n\nTake for example the below string.\n\u3086.\u304f(\u3086.\u308b\u3053(New Byte() { 129, 148, 157, 176, 144, 129, 163, 219 })),\n\nThe namespace is \u201c\u3086\u201d and the functions are \u201c\u304f\u201d and \u201c\u308b\u3053\u201d, with the latter taking a byte array as input and then the resulting output of that function being passed to the former.\n\nStarting with the first function, there are two XOR operations that occur with what looks like two values from the passed in byte array and then a static XOR key.\n\n\nLooking at this function deeper, it uses the last value in the byte array as one of the 3 XOR keys, then adjusts the array in size and begins the decoding loop. Starting at the first byte, it will take this number as the second XOR key and increment it each iteration. The final XOR key is pulled from the GetBytes call on the long string of kanji.\n\nBefore going any further though, can you spot the issue with the function above? It works and successfully decodes the byte array but there is a flaw in codes logic that threw me for a loop when trying to implement the code in Python.\n\nIf you manually XOR those values together (129 [first byte] ^ 214 [last byte] ^ 12375 [first kanji]), the resulting output isn\u2019t what gets returned within the debugger. In fact, it\u2019s not even close which left me scratching my head for a while.\n129^214^12375 = 12288 (0x3000)\n\nInstead, what we end up with is 104 (0x68). It\u2019s clearly wrong though and I assumed I was missing something in what appeared to be a relatively straight forward, par for the course, decoding function. If I XOR the know good result with the two values from the byte array, I end up with 63 (0x3F), otherwise known as \u201c?\u201d.\n\nWhat\u2019s happening is that the GetBytes call is set to use the default system encoding, which in my case is Windows-1252, so the bytes fall outside of the acceptable range and all return as 63 (0x3F), regardless of where the index pointer is in the array. Given this, the only two values I ever need to worry about are within the array itself and I can ignore most of this code.\n\nBelow is a small Python script which will decode the strings passed into it.\ndef decode(a):\n\txorkey = a[-1]\n\ta = a[0:-1]\n\tb = [0] * len(a)\n\tnum = 0\n\tcounter = 0\n\tmaxlength = len(b) - 1\n\twhile counter &lt;= maxlength:\n\t\tb[len(b) - 1 - counter] = chr(a[counter] ^ xorkey ^ 0x3f)\n\t\tcounter += 1\n\treturn \"\".join(b)\n\n&gt;&gt;&gt; a = [129,148,157,176,144,129,163,219]\n&gt;&gt;&gt; decode(a)\n'GetType'\nAs the string successfully decodes with using XOR key 0x3F, it implies it was also encoded with this value initially, so the default code page used by the author when encoding it was also most likely Windows-1252.\n\nThe reason I believe the kanji is more for obfuscation than anything else is because of this and what the XOR key displays, which is nothing but a jumble of random characters without any coherent message.\n\nThis randomness in function and variable names is similar to the techniques they use in later payloads but now with a different character set.\n\nFor the second function, \u201c\u304f\u201d it simply returns a string from the byte array of the previous function.\n\nGoing back to the previously mentioned byte array, it\u2019s quite large and only has one reference inside this code, highlighted below.\n\n\nAfter the byte array is passed to the decoding function, the output is used as input into a new function, \u201c\u3046\u3080\u308c\u3050\u201d, that is responsible for decompressing the data.\n\n\n\nOnce decompressed, the new data is returned in a byte array.\n\nAt this point I copied out the list of integers for the byte array and ran it through the decoding Python function and decompressed the it with the zlib library into the next payload.\nfh = open(\"output\", \"w\")\nfh.write(zlib.decompress(decode(a), -15))\nfh.close()\n\nLooking at the new file shows that it is a DLL named \u201crp.dll\u201d.\n\n\nThis was also a .NET file and we can load it into dnSpy for further analysis; however, before doing that I\u2019ll go over the final part of the first packer.\nDim objectValue As Object = RuntimeHelpers.GetObjectValue(NewLateBinding.LateGet(Nothing,CType(NewLateBinding.LateGet(Nothing, \u201cSystem.Type\u201d,\u201cGetType\u201d, \u201cSystem.Reflection.Assembly\u201d, Nothing, Nothing, Nothing), Type), \u201cLoad\u201d, BINARY_ARRAY, Nothing, Nothing, Nothing))\n\nDim objectValue2 As Object = RuntimeHelpers.GetObjectValue(NewLateBinding.LateGet(RuntimeHelpers.GetObjectValue(objectValue), Nothing, \"GetType\", \"\u3068\u3080\u66ae.\u3068\u3080\u66ae\", Nothing, Nothing, Nothing))\n\nNewLateBinding.LateGet(Nothing, CType(NewLateBinding.LateGet(Nothing, Type.[GetType](\"System.Type\", \"GetType\", \"System.Activator\", Nothing, Nothing, Nothing), Type), \"CreateInstance\", New Object() { RuntimeHelpers.GetObjectValue(objectValue2) }, Nothing, Nothing, Nothing)\n\nI\u2019ve cleaned up the encoded strings so you can see what it\u2019s doing but effectively, it takes the DLL assembly, loads it, and calls the main function, \u201c\u3068\u3080\u66ae.\u3068\u3080\u66ae\u201d, within it.\n\nThis DLL uses the same byte array string obfuscation as the initial executable.\n\n\nIn the above image, you can see it begins by checking whether the file \u201c\\\\Products\\\\WinDecode.exe\u201d exists and then will create the \u201c\\\\Products\\\\\u201d directory if it does not. After that it will enumerate processes to kill, delete files, establish itself in the registry for persistence and other characteristics typical of this malware.\n\n\n\nBut, eventually during the execution, you\u2019ll end up at the next part of the unpacking code.\nDim obj2 As Object = \u3068\u3080\u66ae.\u308c\u306a\u3064(Me.\u307e\u3053\u3046\u306b(Me.\u3053\u306a\u304d(Me.\u308c\u306a())))\nDim \u3046\u3072\u786f\u308b As \u3046\u3072\u786f\u308b = New \u3046\u3072\u786f\u308b()\nReturn \u3046\u3072\u786f\u308b.\u3046(\"Nothing\", String.Empty, CType(obj2, Byte()), True)\n\nThe first line calls multiple functions - starting on the far right is \u201c\u308c\u306a\u201d. This function can be seen below and creates an object from a PNG file in the resources section of the DLL.\n\n\nPicture Time\nThe PNG itself doesn\u2019t visually show anything of note but static.\n\n\n\nThe next function \u201c\u3053\u306a\u304d\u201d is a bit more interesting.\n\n\n\nThis loads the image as a bitmap and then it will read the pixels in a certain order to build an array from the values for Red, Green, and Blue that get returned.\n\nFor example, if you look at the bottom left of the image (0,192), you will see a dark green with the hex value 0x1AE2C.\n\n\n\nThe first entries in the array would be 0x2C (Blue), 0xAE (Green), 0x1 (Red)\n\nTo unpack this, I once again re-wrote the code in Python and used the Python Imaging Library (PIL) to extract the bytes. This particular image is 192x192 pixels and 24bits per pixel (3 bytes \u2013 RGB) and it iterates over each pixel from left to right, bottom to top, for the array of data.\nfrom PIL import Image\nim = Image.open(\"\/Users\/pickleRICK\/19.png\")\n\ndef imparse(a):\n\twidth, height = im.size\n\tcounter = 0\n\tb = [0] * (width * height * 3)\n\tfor y in range(height - 1, -1, -1):\n\t\tfor x in range(0,width):\n\t\t\tpixel = im.getpixel((x,y))\n\t\t\tb[counter * 3 + 2] = pixel[0] # R\n\t\t\tb[counter * 3 + 1] = pixel[1] # G\n\t\t\tb[counter * 3 + 0] = pixel[2] # B\n\t\t\tcounter += 1\n\twhile b[-1] == 0:\n\t\tdel b[-1]\n\treturn b\n\nAfter it returns, the byte array gets passed to the now familiar decode function and then the deflate function.\n&gt;&gt;&gt; dec = imparse(im)\n&gt;&gt;&gt; dec\n[44, 174, 1, 0, 237, 11, 8, 125, 109, 41, 15, \u2026\n\n&gt;&gt;&gt; dec = decode(dec)\n&gt;&gt;&gt; dec\n'\\xec\\xbd\\tx\\x1c\\xc5\\x95\\x00\\xdcs\\xf59#\\xa9\\xa6G \u2026\n\n&gt;&gt;&gt; dec = zlib.decompress(dec,-15)\n&gt;&gt;&gt; dec\n'MZ\\x90\\x00\\x03\\x00\\x00\\x00\\x04\\x00\\x00\\x00\\xff\\xff\\x00 \u2026\n\nAs you can see, we have the MZ header and the next binary.\n\nWithin the DLL are additional functions which handle executing the new payload and I\u2019ve gone ahead and decoded some of the native API\u2019s they use to show how they carry out activity.\n\nThe final payload\nArrival of the last binary \u2013 another .NET application called \u201cRII9DKFR5LC4Y669MLOA2C50SFLPHZBN61CZ160Z.exe\u201d. If you read any of the posts mentioned earlier on the analysis of AgentTesla, then this will look familiar.\n\n\n\nFunction and variable names are encoded with Unicode values in the range of 0x200B-0x200E. Strings are decrypted by, in this sample, function \u201cKMBHFDXSELJYYLVK\\u3002\u201d.\n\n\n\nThis function uses a hardcoded password and salt to derive a key from the SHA1 hashing algorithm as implemented by Microsoft (modified PBKDF1). Afterwards, it uses the key and hardcoded IV to decrypt the string with AES-CBC.\n\n\n\nA quick Google for that IV shows hundreds of results for it, with most revolving around an encryption example that was used as the base for this function \u2013 it even copies the examples variable names.\n\nWhat I found interesting here is that none of these values ever change sample to sample. Even going back to the samples in the write-ups on AgentTesla from over 6 months ago, I was able to decrypt their base64 strings listed in the blog. This confirms the same values are in use and likely hard coded into the builder for AgentTesla.\n\nGiven that everything is static then, it\u2019s fairly trivial to extract all of the base64 encoded strings, decrypt them, and look for interesting IoC\u2019s.\nfrom Crypto.Cipher import AES\ndef stringdecrypt(a):\n\tstring = base64.b64decode(a)\n\tiv = \"@1B2c3D4e5F6g7H8\"\n\tkey = \"\\x34\\x88\\x6D\\x5B\\x09\\x7A\\x94\\x19\\x78\\xD0\\xE3\\x8b\\x1b\\x5c\\xa3\\x29\\x60\\x74\\x6a\\x5e\\x5d\\x64\\x87\\x11\\xb1\\x2c\\x67\\xaa\\x5b\\x3a\\x8e\\xbf\" #to 6a\/5e for first iteration\n\tcleartext = AES.new(key[0:32], AES.MODE_CBC, iv).decrypt(string)\n\treturn cleartext\n\nfh = open(\"extractedb64\")\ncontent = fh.readlines()\nfh.close()\nfor i in content:\n\ttry:\n\t\tdec = stringdecrypt(i)\n\t\tprint \"%s | %s\" % (i.strip(),dec.strip())\n\texcept:\n\t\tpass\n\nWhat we end up with is a long list of values like the below.\ncWUeT8dJU4KfzxUEgGflzQ== | temp\ny9\/s0\/2Soj9dWZ7YCF9viw== | \\des_date.txt\nhQ1zQ5Cg31OSE+BZ2Os36w== | 2017-08-25\ncWUeT8dJU4KfzxUEgGflzQ== | temp\ny9\/s0\/2Soj9dWZ7YCF9viw== | You can also see where the corresponding base64 is within the code and then use dnSpy to obtain further context on how AgentTesla utilizes these values.\n\nFor example, below is something that stood out as interesting almost immediately.\n4nmIR8y7iw8axs2u6GfIQ8f\/7fSpMKvqD0ODaew16nI= | mylogbox3h@gmail.com\n5XDX6cForslWY791UzW+zw== | sammy1990\nwf990RzBidRdPMgWIckJ2g== | smtp.gmail.com\n\nPivoting to these values in dnSpy will land you in a function that seems responsible for sending the stolen data back to the attacker.\n\nPivoting on a hunch\nAt this point, I\u2019ve accomplished the goal I set out for \u2013 covering the packing techniques used by the current version of AgentTesla, offering some code to automate unpacking and decrypt some configuration data.\n\nBut why stop when you\u2019re ahead?\n\nI like to Google static values and constants when analyzing malware because you can usually find some interesting stuff \u2013 configurations, forums, accounts, panels, etc. When I began searching for the file \u201cone.jpeg.png.exe\u201d I stumbled across a site, \u201cb-f-v[.]info\u201d, which hosts various versions of this keylogger.\n\n\n\nThey all function in the same way but the image that displays is related to the first part of the file name. The images are various sizes so the decoding would be different for each; however, the code shown previously will grab the correct Width and Height for building the array.\n\nAlso take note of the dates and when they were modified. The sample covered in this blog was seen on August 29th, just two days before these were created \u2013 so the person or group behind these appear to be actively creating new versions to send out. I confirmed in these samples we find the same SMTP credentials.\nConclusion\nHopefully this overview of their packing techniques, along with the scripts to unpack each phase, prove helpful to others when looking at AgentTesla. Given its recent spikes in popularity, it\u2019s likely not going anywhere anytime soon so the more knowledge you have of the threat, the better you can defend yourself.\n\nYou can continue to track     /**
 * String.prototype.replaceAll() polyfill
 * https://gomakethings.com/how-to-replace-a-section-of-a-string-with-another-one-with-vanilla-js/
 * @author Chris Ferdinandi
 * @license MIT
 */
if (!String.prototype.replaceAll) {
	String.prototype.replaceAll = function(str, newStr){
		// If a regex pattern
		if (Object.prototype.toString.call(str).toLowerCase() === '[object regexp]') {
			return this.replace(str, newStr);
		}
		// If a string
		return this.replace(new RegExp(str, 'g'), newStr);
	};
}

	function getCookie(cname) {
	 	var name = cname + "=";
  		var decodedCookie = decodeURIComponent(document.cookie);
		var ca = decodedCookie.split(';');
  		for(var i = 0; i <ca.length; i++) {
    			var c = ca[i];
    			while (c.charAt(0) == ' ') {
     				 c = c.substring(1);
    			}
    			if (c.indexOf(name) == 0) {
    				 return c.substring(name.length, c.length);
    			}
  		}
  		return "";
	}

	var referer = "";//sessionStorage.container;
	var pcontainer = sessionStorage.getItem("container");
	var searchResultsPagePath = "";
	if(((pcontainer) && pcontainer.indexOf('Prisma')!=-1)){
	    referer = 'Prisma' ;
	}        
        else if(((pcontainer) && pcontainer.indexOf('Cortex')!=-1)){
            if( pcontainer.indexOf('CloudCortex') != -1){
                referer = 'CloudCortex' ;
            }
            else{
                referer = 'Cortex' ;
            }	    
	}
        else if(((pcontainer) && pcontainer.indexOf('Sase')!=-1)){
	    referer = 'Sase' ;
	}
	else if(((pcontainer) && pcontainer.indexOf('Unit')!=-1)){
	    referer = 'Unit' ;
	}
	else if(((pcontainer) && pcontainer.indexOf('Ngfw')!=-1)){
	    referer = 'Ngfw' ;
	}
        var fromRef = document.referrer;
        var nContainer = getCookie("navContainer");
        if(nContainer){//If user is coming from main site, we need to reset the container		
		if(fromRef  && fromRef.indexOf("prismacloud.io")!=-1){
                        referer = 'Prisma' ;
                        sessionStorage.setItem("container","Prisma");
                } else if(fromRef.indexOf("paloaltonetworks.com")!=-1 || fromRef.indexOf("paloaltonetworks.jp")!=-1 ){
                        if(nContainer.indexOf('Prisma') != -1){
                            referer = 'Prisma' ;
                            sessionStorage.setItem("container","Prisma");
                        }
                        if(nContainer.indexOf('Cortex') != -1){
                            if( nContainer.indexOf('CloudCortex') != -1){
                                referer = 'CloudCortex';
                                sessionStorage.setItem("container","CloudCortex");
                            }
                            else{
                                referer = 'Cortex';
                                sessionStorage.setItem("container","Cortex");
                            }                            
                        }
			if(nContainer.indexOf('Sase') != -1){
                            referer = 'Sase' ;
                            sessionStorage.setItem("container","Sase");
                        }
			if(nContainer.indexOf('Unit') != -1){
                            referer = 'Unit' ;
                            sessionStorage.setItem("container","Unit");
                        }

                            referer = 'Ngfw' ;
                            sessionStorage.setItem("container","Ngfw");
                        }
			document.cookie = 'navContainer=; path=/; domain=.paloaltonetworks.com; expires=' + new Date(0).toUTCString();
		}
	}
if(referer != "Prisma" && referer != "CloudCortex" && referer != "Cortex" && referer != "Sase" && referer != "Unit" && referer != "Ngfw") {
    referer = 'Unit' ;
    sessionStorage.setItem("container","Unit");
}
function callMainSitePrismaNavHTML(){
    
    var referrer_domain = 'https://www.paloaltonetworks.com';
    sessionStorage.setItem("domain",referrer_domain);
    if(referer == 'Prisma'){
        //var menu_url = referrer_domain+'/_jcr_content/globals/cleanHeaderPrisma.prismaRenderer.html';
        var menu_url = 'https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/themes/unit42-v6/unit-nav-renderer.php?type=prisma&domain=en';
        searchResultsPagePath = referrer_domain+"/search/prismasearch";
        }
    if(referer == 'CloudCortex'){
        var menu_url = 'https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/themes/unit42-v6/unit-nav-renderer.php?type=cortexcloud&domain=en';
        searchResultsPagePath = referrer_domain+"/search/cortexcloudsearch";	
    }
    if(referer == 'Cortex'){
        //var menu_url = referrer_domain+'/_jcr_content/globals/cleanHeaderCortex.cortexRenderer.html';	
        var menu_url = 'https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/themes/unit42-v6/unit-nav-renderer.php?type=cortex&domain=en';	
        searchResultsPagePath = referrer_domain+"/search/cortexsearch";	
    }
    if(referer == 'Sase'){
        //var menu_url = referrer_domain+'/_jcr_content/globals/cleanHeaderSase.saseRenderer.html';
        var menu_url = 'https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/themes/unit42-v6/unit-nav-renderer.php?type=sase&domain=en';
        searchResultsPagePath = referrer_domain+"/search/sasesearch";
    }
    if(referer == 'Unit'){
        var menu_url = 'https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/themes/unit42-v6/unit-nav-renderer.php?type=unit42&domain=en';
        searchResultsPagePath = referrer_domain+"/content/pan/en_US/search/unit42search";
		    }
    if(referer == 'Ngfw'){
        //var menu_url = 'https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/themes/unit42-v6/ngfw-cdss-nav-renderer.php';
        var menu_url = 'https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/themes/unit42-v6/unit-nav-renderer.php?type=ngfw&domain=en'; 
        searchResultsPagePath = referrer_domain+"/search/ngfwcdsssearch";
    }
    httpGet(menu_url,'menu_html');
    document.getElementById('main-nav-menu-cont').removeAttribute("style");
}
function addStyle(styles) {            
    /* Create style document */
    var css = document.createElement('style');
    css.type = 'text/css';

    if (css.styleSheet) 
        css.styleSheet.cssText = styles;
    else 
        css.appendChild(document.createTextNode(styles));

    /* Append style to the tag name */
    document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(css);
}
function httpGet(theUrl,req_type) {
    if (window.XMLHttpRequest) { // code for IE7+, Firefox, Chrome, Opera, Safari
        xmlhttp=new XMLHttpRequest();
    } else {// code for IE6, IE5
        xmlhttp=new ActiveXObject("Microsoft.XMLHTTP");
    }
    xmlhttp.onreadystatechange=function()
    {
        if (xmlhttp.readyState==4 && xmlhttp.status==200)
        {
            if(req_type == 'menu_html'){
            var nav_text = xmlhttp.responseText.replaceAll('https://static.cloud.coveo.com/searchui/v2.9159/js/CoveoJsSearch.Lazy.min.js', '');
            nav_text = nav_text.replaceAll('src="/', 'src="'+maindomain_lang+'/');
            nav_text = nav_text.replaceAll("'/content", "'"+maindomain_lang+"/content");
                        
            document.getElementById("PAN_2021_NAV_ASYNC").innerHTML = nav_text.replaceAll('href="/', 'href="'+maindomain_lang+'/');

            var lozad_back = document.getElementsByClassName('lozad-background');
            Array.prototype.forEach.call(lozad_back, function(el) {
            // Do stuff here
            var el_back_img_path = el.getAttribute('data-background-image');
            var first_pos = el_back_img_path.indexOf("'");
            var last_pos = el_back_img_path.indexOf("'",first_pos+1);
            el_back_img_path = el_back_img_path.substring(first_pos+1,last_pos);
            el.setAttribute("data-background-image",main_site_url+el_back_img_path);
            });
            const observer_lozad = lozad('.lozad, .lozad-background'); // lazy loads elements with default selector as '.lozad'
            observer_lozad.observe();
            }
            if(req_type == 'head_inline_css'){
            addStyle(xmlhttp.responseText);
            }
        }
    }
    xmlhttp.open("GET", theUrl, true );
    xmlhttp.send();    
}    
    
if(referer == 'Prisma' || referer == 'CloudCortex' || referer == 'Cortex' || referer == 'Sase' || referer == 'Unit' || referer == 'Ngfw'){
    const article = document.querySelector('#PAN_2021_NAV_ASYNC');
    if(referer == 'Prisma'){
        article.dataset.type = 'prisma';
        $('#PAN_2021_NAV_ASYNC').removeClass('default').addClass('defaultRedesigned');
    }
    else if(referer == 'CloudCortex'){
        article.dataset.type = 'cloudcortex';
    }
    else if(referer == 'Cortex'){
        article.dataset.type = 'cortex';
    }
    else if(referer == 'Sase'){
        article.dataset.type = 'sase';
    }
    else if(referer == 'Unit'){
        article.dataset.type = 'unit';
    }
    else if(referer == 'Ngfw'){
        article.dataset.type = 'ngfw';
    }
    //set class to default
    if(referer == 'Unit' || referer == 'Ngfw'){
        $('#PAN_2021_NAV_ASYNC').removeClass('default').addClass('defaultRedesigned');
    }
    callMainSitePrismaNavHTML();
}

      </section> 
    <section class="section blog-contents"> 
      <div class="pa blog-editor">
        <div class="l-container">
          <div class="be__wrapper">
            <div class="be__contents">
              <div class="be__contents-wrapper">
                <p>AgentTesla is a fairly popular key logger built using the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/.NET_Framework">Microsoft .NET Framework</a> and has shown a substantial rise in usage over the past few months.</p>
<p><img  class="alignnone size-full wp-image-42816 lozad"  data-src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_1.png" alt="agenttesla_1" width="811" height="379" data-srcset="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_1.png 811w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_1-300x140.png 300w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_1-768x359.png 768w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_1-370x173.png 370w" sizes="(max-width: 811px) 100vw, 811px" /></p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p>It offers all of the standard features of a keylogger but goes beyond the typical confines of this type of software. One particular feature of interest is the custom packer it uses to hide the primary AgentTesla binary. Packers allow for a binary to essentially be wrapped in another binary to mask the original one from detection.</p>
<p>There are a number of excellent blogs out there covering <a href="https://www.google.com/url?sa=t&amp;rct=j&amp;q=&amp;esrc=s&amp;source=web&amp;cd=11&amp;cad=rja&amp;uact=8&amp;ved=0ahUKEwiuguS2j4PWAhXkzIMKHWy1Ass4ChAWCCcwAA&amp;url=https%3A%2F%2Fblog.fortinet.com%2F2017%2F06%2F28%2Fin-depth-analysis-of-net-malware-javaupdtr&amp;usg=AFQjCNG0sqBMWMMIo">AgentTesla’s functionality</a> and it’s <a href="https://blogs.forcepoint.com/security-labs/part-two-camouflage-netting">various obfuscations</a>, but having I recently unpacked a sample and wanted to focus on this particular function and provide some helpful tools to aide in unpacking it.</p>
<p>For this analysis, I’ll be using a PE32 version AgentTesla file seen in the wild on August 29<sup>th</sup> with hash “ca29bd44fc1c4ec031eadf89fb2894bbe646bc0cafb6242a7631f7404ef7d15c”. You’ll find AgentTesla delivered commonly via <a href="https://cysinfo.com/agent-tesla-new-spyware-variant-plucked-hackers-arena/">phishing documents</a> that usually contain VBA macros to download and run a file – like the one in question.</p>
<p>As it’s a commercial product, you’ll find a lot of variety in the initial carrier files that deliver the AgentTesla binary; however, at some point you’ll find yourself with a PE.</p>
<h2>Thus, begins the journey…</h2>
<p>I suppose the first layer of obfuscation really begins with the file itself, called “one.jpeg.png.exe” and an icon of a JPG trying to create an illusion of legitimacy.</p>
<p><img  class="alignnone size-full wp-image-42840 lozad"  data-src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_2.png" alt="agenttesla_2" width="288" height="258" /></p>
<p>This is a common technique to fool people and they’ve taken it one step further by opening an image when you execute the binary.</p>
<p><img  class="alignnone size-full wp-image-42864 lozad"  data-src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_3.png" alt="agenttesla_3" width="681" height="706" data-srcset="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_3.png 681w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_3-289x300.png 289w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_3-370x384.png 370w" sizes="(max-width: 681px) 100vw, 681px" /></p>
<p>The first executable is a .NET application, which is no surprise since AgentTesla is very well known for being a .NET key logger. To analyze .NET applications, I prefer to use the application dnSpy and, once loading up this sample, we can see there is only one namespace of interest with a handful of functions and a byte array.</p>
<p><img  class="alignnone size-full wp-image-42888 lozad"  data-src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_4.png" alt="agenttesla_4" width="629" height="640" data-srcset="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_4.png 629w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_4-295x300.png 295w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_4-370x376.png 370w" sizes="(max-width: 629px) 100vw, 629px" /></p>
<p>The Japanese kanji stands out at first glance but I believe this is less about language and more about being a form of obfuscation – I’ll explain why shortly.</p>
<p>Looking at the Main() function shows a pattern of multiple calls to two other functions.</p>
<p><img  class="alignnone size-full wp-image-42912 lozad"  data-src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_5.png" alt="agenttesla_5" width="974" height="238" data-srcset="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_5.png 974w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_5-900x220.png 900w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_5-300x73.png 300w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_5-768x188.png 768w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_5-370x90.png 370w" sizes="(max-width: 974px) 100vw, 974px" /></p>
<p>Take for example the below string.</p><!-- Crayon Syntax Highlighter v_2.7.2_beta -->

		<div id="crayon-68c8c6028f9e0855127858" class="crayon-syntax crayon-theme-classic crayon-font-monaco crayon-os-pc print-yes notranslate" data-settings=" minimize scroll-mouseover wrap" style=" margin-top: 12px; margin-bottom: 12px; font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important;">

ゆ.く(ゆ.るこ(New Byte() { 129, 148, 157, 176, 144, 129, 163, 219 })),</textarea></div>

<!-- [Format Time: 0.0006 seconds] -->
<p>The namespace is “ゆ” and the functions are “く” and “るこ”, with the latter taking a byte array as input and then the resulting output of that function being passed to the former.</p>
<p>Starting with the first function, there are two XOR operations that occur with what looks like two values from the passed in byte array and then a static XOR key.<br />
<img  class="alignnone size-full wp-image-42936 lozad"  data-src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_6.png" alt="agenttesla_6" width="974" height="281" data-srcset="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_6.png 974w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_6-900x260.png 900w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_6-300x87.png 300w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_6-768x222.png 768w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_6-370x107.png 370w" sizes="(max-width: 974px) 100vw, 974px" /></p>
<p>Looking at this function deeper, it uses the last value in the byte array as one of the 3 XOR keys, then adjusts the array in size and begins the decoding loop. Starting at the first byte, it will take this number as the second XOR key and increment it each iteration. The final XOR key is pulled from the GetBytes call on the long string of kanji.</p>
<p>Before going any further though, can you spot the issue with the function above? It works and successfully decodes the byte array but there is a flaw in codes logic that threw me for a loop when trying to implement the code in Python.</p>
<p>If you manually XOR those values together (129 [first byte] ^ 214 [last byte] ^ 12375 [first kanji]), the resulting output isn’t what gets returned within the debugger. In fact, it’s not even close which left me scratching my head for a while.</p><!-- Crayon Syntax Highlighter v_2.7.2_beta -->

		<div id="crayon-68c8c6028f9eb972727162" class="crayon-syntax crayon-theme-classic crayon-font-monaco crayon-os-pc print-yes notranslate" data-settings=" minimize scroll-mouseover wrap" style=" margin-top: 12px; margin-bottom: 12px; font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important;">
		
			<div class="crayon-toolbar" data-settings=" mouseover overlay hide delay" style="font-size: 12px !important;height: 18px !important; line-height: 18px !important;"><span class="crayon-title"></span>
			<div class="crayon-tools" style="font-size: 12px !important;height: 18px !important; line-height: 18px !important;"><div class="crayon-button crayon-nums-button" title="Toggle Line Numbers"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div><div class="crayon-button crayon-plain-button" title="Toggle Plain Code"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div><div class="crayon-button crayon-wrap-button" title="Toggle Line Wrap"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div><div class="crayon-button crayon-expand-button" title="Expand Code"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div><div class="crayon-button crayon-copy-button" title="Copy"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div><div class="crayon-button crayon-popup-button" title="Open Code In New Window"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div></div></div>
			<div class="crayon-info" style="min-height: 16.8px !important; line-height: 16.8px !important;"></div>
			<div class="crayon-plain-wrap"><textarea  class="crayon-plain print-no" data-settings="dblclick" readonly style="-moz-tab-size:4; -o-tab-size:4; -webkit-tab-size:4; tab-size:4; font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important;">
129^214^12375 = 12288 (0x3000)</textarea></div>
			<div class="crayon-main" style="">
				<table class="crayon-table">
					<tr class="crayon-row">
				<td class="crayon-nums " data-settings="show">
					<div class="crayon-nums-content" style="font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important;"><div class="crayon-num" data-line="crayon-68c8c6028f9eb972727162-1">1</div></div>
				</td>
						<td class="crayon-code"><div class="crayon-pre" style="font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important; -moz-tab-size:4; -o-tab-size:4; -webkit-tab-size:4; tab-size:4;"><div class="crayon-line" id="crayon-68c8c6028f9eb972727162-1"><span class="crayon-cn">129</span>^<span class="crayon-cn">214</span>^<span class="crayon-cn">12375</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span>=<span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-cn">12288</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-sy">(</span><span class="crayon-cn">0x3000</span><span class="crayon-sy">)</span></div></div></td>

<!-- [Format Time: 0.0001 seconds] -->
<p>Instead, what we end up with is 104 (0x68). It’s clearly wrong though and I assumed I was missing something in what appeared to be a relatively straight forward, par for the course, decoding function. If I XOR the know good result with the two values from the byte array, I end up with 63 (0x3F), otherwise known as “?”.</p>
<p>What’s happening is that the GetBytes call is set to use the default system encoding, which in my case is Windows-1252, so the bytes fall outside of the acceptable range and all return as 63 (0x3F), regardless of where the index pointer is in the array. Given this, the only two values I ever need to worry about are within the array itself and I can ignore most of this code.</p>
<p>Below is a small Python script which will decode the strings passed into it.</p><!-- Crayon Syntax Highlighter v_2.7.2_beta -->

		<div id="crayon-68c8c6028f9ef071513690" class="crayon-syntax crayon-theme-classic crayon-font-monaco crayon-os-pc print-yes notranslate" data-settings=" minimize scroll-mouseover wrap" style=" margin-top: 12px; margin-bottom: 12px; font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important;">
		
			<div class="crayon-toolbar" data-settings=" mouseover overlay hide delay" style="font-size: 12px !important;height: 18px !important; line-height: 18px !important;"><span class="crayon-title"></span>
			<div class="crayon-tools" style="font-size: 12px !important;height: 18px !important; line-height: 18px !important;"><div class="crayon-button crayon-nums-button" title="Toggle Line Numbers"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div><div class="crayon-button crayon-plain-button" title="Toggle Plain Code"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div><div class="crayon-button crayon-wrap-button" title="Toggle Line Wrap"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div><div class="crayon-button crayon-expand-button" title="Expand Code"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div><div class="crayon-button crayon-copy-button" title="Copy"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div><div class="crayon-button crayon-popup-button" title="Open Code In New Window"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div></div></div>
			<div class="crayon-info" style="min-height: 16.8px !important; line-height: 16.8px !important;"></div>
			<div class="crayon-plain-wrap"><textarea  class="crayon-plain print-no" data-settings="dblclick" readonly style="-moz-tab-size:4; -o-tab-size:4; -webkit-tab-size:4; tab-size:4; font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important;">
def decode(a):
	xorkey = a[-1]
	a = a[0:-1]
	b = [0] * len(a)
	num = 0
	counter = 0
	maxlength = len(b) - 1
	while counter &lt;= maxlength:
		b[len(b) - 1 - counter] = chr(a[counter] ^ xorkey ^ 0x3f)
		counter += 1
	return "".join(b)

&gt;&gt;&gt; a = [129,148,157,176,144,129,163,219]
&gt;&gt;&gt; decode(a)
'GetType'</textarea></div>
			<div class="crayon-main" style="">
				<table class="crayon-table">
					<tr class="crayon-row">
				<td class="crayon-nums " data-settings="show">
					<div class="crayon-nums-content" style="font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important;"><div class="crayon-num" data-line="crayon-68c8c6028f9ef071513690-1">1</div><div class="crayon-num crayon-striped-num" data-line="crayon-68c8c6028f9ef071513690-2">2</div><div class="crayon-num" data-line="crayon-68c8c6028f9ef071513690-3">3</div><div class="crayon-num crayon-striped-num" data-line="crayon-68c8c6028f9ef071513690-4">4</div><div class="crayon-num" data-line="crayon-68c8c6028f9ef071513690-5">5</div><div class="crayon-num crayon-striped-num" data-line="crayon-68c8c6028f9ef071513690-6">6</div><div class="crayon-num" data-line="crayon-68c8c6028f9ef071513690-7">7</div><div class="crayon-num crayon-striped-num" data-line="crayon-68c8c6028f9ef071513690-8">8</div><div class="crayon-num" data-line="crayon-68c8c6028f9ef071513690-9">9</div><div class="crayon-num crayon-striped-num" data-line="crayon-68c8c6028f9ef071513690-10">10</div><div class="crayon-num" data-line="crayon-68c8c6028f9ef071513690-11">11</div><div class="crayon-num crayon-striped-num" data-line="crayon-68c8c6028f9ef071513690-12">12</div><div class="crayon-num" data-line="crayon-68c8c6028f9ef071513690-13">13</div><div class="crayon-num crayon-striped-num" data-line="crayon-68c8c6028f9ef071513690-14">14</div><div class="crayon-num" data-line="crayon-68c8c6028f9ef071513690-15">15</div></div>
				</td>
						<td class="crayon-code"><div class="crayon-pre" style="font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important; -moz-tab-size:4; -o-tab-size:4; -webkit-tab-size:4; tab-size:4;"><div class="crayon-line" id="crayon-68c8c6028f9ef071513690-1"><span class="crayon-e">def </span><span class="crayon-e">decode</span><span class="crayon-sy">(</span><span class="crayon-i">a</span><span class="crayon-sy">)</span><span class="crayon-sy">:</span></div><div class="crayon-line crayon-striped-line" id="crayon-68c8c6028f9ef071513690-2"><span class="crayon-h">	</span><span 		</div>
<!-- [Format Time: 0.0003 seconds] -->
<p>As the string successfully decodes with using XOR key 0x3F, it implies it was also encoded with this value initially, so the default code page used by the author when encoding it was also most likely Windows-1252.</p>
<p>The reason I believe the kanji is more for obfuscation than anything else is because of this and what the XOR key displays, which is nothing but a jumble of random characters without any coherent message.</p>
<p>This randomness in function and variable names is similar to the techniques they use in later payloads but now with a different character set.</p>
<p>For the second function, “く” it simply returns a string from the byte array of the previous function.</p>
<p>Going back to the previously mentioned byte array, it’s quite large and only has one reference inside this code, highlighted below.<br />
<img  class="alignnone size-full wp-image-42960 lozad"  data-src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_7.png" alt="agenttesla_7" width="972" height="101" data-srcset="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_7.png 972w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_7-900x94.png 900w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_7-300x31.png 300w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_7-768x80.png 768w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_7-370x38.png 370w" sizes="(max-width: 972px) 100vw, 972px" /></p>
<p>After the byte array is passed to the decoding function, the output is used as input into a new function, “うむれぐ”, that is responsible for decompressing the data.</p>
<p><img  class="alignnone size-full wp-image-42984 lozad"  data-src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_8.png" alt="agenttesla_8" width="974" height="235" data-srcset="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_8.png 974w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_8-900x217.png 900w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_8-300x72.png 300w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_8-768x185.png 768w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_8-370x89.png 370w" sizes="(max-width: 974px) 100vw, 974px" /></p>
<p>Once decompressed, the new data is returned in a byte array.</p>
<p>At this point I copied out the list of integers for the byte array and ran it through the decoding Python function and decompressed the it with the zlib library into the next payload.</p><!-- Crayon Syntax Highlighter v_2.7.2_beta -->

<p>Looking at the new file shows that it is a DLL named “rp.dll”.<br />
<img  class="alignnone size-full wp-image-43008 lozad"  data-src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_9.png" alt="agenttesla_9" width="915" height="1092" data-srcset="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_9.png 915w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_9-900x1074.png 900w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_9-251x300.png 251w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_9-768x917.png 768w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_9-858x1024.png 858w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_9-370x442.png 370w" sizes="(max-width: 915px) 100vw, 915px" /></p>
<p>This was also a .NET file and we can load it into dnSpy for further analysis; however, before doing that I’ll go over the final part of the first packer.</p><!-- Crayon Syntax Highlighter v_2.7.2_beta -->

<!-- [Format Time: 0.0003 seconds] -->
<p>I’ve cleaned up the encoded strings so you can see what it’s doing but effectively, it takes the DLL assembly, loads it, and calls the main function, “とむ暮.とむ暮”, within it.</p>
<p>This DLL uses the same byte array string obfuscation as the initial executable.<br />
<img  class="alignnone size-full wp-image-43032 lozad"  data-src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_10.png" alt="agenttesla_10" width="974" height="379" data-srcset="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_10.png 974w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_10-900x350.png 900w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_10-300x117.png 300w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_10-768x299.png 768w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_10-370x144.png 370w" sizes="(max-width: 974px) 100vw, 974px" /></p>
<p>In the above image, you can see it begins by checking whether the file “\\Products\\WinDecode.exe” exists and then will create the “\\Products\\” directory if it does not. After that it will enumerate processes to kill, delete files, establish itself in the registry for persistence and other characteristics typical of this malware.</p>
<p><img  class="alignnone size-full wp-image-43056 lozad"  data-src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_11.png" alt="agenttesla_11" width="974" height="275" data-srcset="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_11.png 974w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_11-900x254.png 900w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_11-300x85.png 300w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_11-768x217.png 768w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_11-370x104.png 370w" sizes="(max-width: 974px) 100vw, 974px" /></p>
<p>But, eventually during the execution, you’ll end up at the next part of the unpacking code.</p><!-- Crayon Syntax Highlighter v_2.7.2_beta -->

<!-- [Format Time: 0.0001 seconds] -->
<p>The first line calls multiple functions - starting on the far right is “れな”. This function can be seen below and creates an object from a PNG file in the resources section of the DLL.</p>
<p><img  class="alignnone size-full wp-image-43080 lozad"  data-src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_12.png" alt="agenttesla_12" width="974" height="75" data-srcset="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_12.png 974w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_12-900x69.png 900w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_12-300x23.png 300w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_12-768x59.png 768w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_12-370x28.png 370w" sizes="(max-width: 974px) 100vw, 974px" /></p>
<h2>Picture Time</h2>
<p>The PNG itself doesn’t visually show anything of note but static.</p>
<p><img  class="alignnone size-full wp-image-43104 lozad"  data-src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_13.png" alt="agenttesla_13" width="975" height="468" data-srcset="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_13.png 975w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_13-900x432.png 900w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_13-300x144.png 300w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_13-768x369.png 768w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_13-370x178.png 370w" sizes="(max-width: 975px) 100vw, 975px" /></p>
<p>The next function “こなき” is a bit more interesting.</p>
<p><img  class="alignnone size-full wp-image-43128 lozad"  data-src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_14.png" alt="agenttesla_14" width="965" height="421" data-srcset="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_14.png 965w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_14-900x393.png 900w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_14-300x131.png 300w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_14-768x335.png 768w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_14-370x161.png 370w" sizes="(max-width: 965px) 100vw, 965px" /></p>
<p>This loads the image as a bitmap and then it will read the pixels in a certain order to build an array from the values for Red, Green, and Blue that get returned.</p>
<p>For example, if you look at the bottom left of the image (0,192), you will see a dark green with the hex value 0x1AE2C.</p>
<p><img  class="alignnone size-full wp-image-43152 lozad"  data-src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_15.png" alt="agenttesla_15" width="603" height="529" data-srcset="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_15.png 603w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_15-300x263.png 300w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_15-370x325.png 370w" sizes="(max-width: 603px) 100vw, 603px" /></p>
<p>The first entries in the array would be 0x2C (Blue), 0xAE (Green), 0x1 (Red)</p>
<p>To unpack this, I once again re-wrote the code in Python and used the Python Imaging Library (PIL) to extract the bytes. This particular image is 192x192 pixels and 24bits per pixel (3 bytes – RGB) and it iterates over each pixel from left to right, bottom to top, for the array of data.</p><!-- Crayon Syntax Highlighter v_2.7.2_beta -->

		<div id="crayon-68c8c6028f9fa273820075" class="crayon-syntax crayon-theme-classic crayon-font-monaco crayon-os-pc print-yes notranslate" data-settings=" minimize scroll-mouseover wrap" style=" margin-top: 12px; margin-bottom: 12px; font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important;">
		
			<div class="crayon-toolbar" data-settings=" mouseover overlay hide delay" style="font-size: 12px !important;height: 18px !important; line-height: 18px !important;"><span class="crayon-title"></span>
			<div class="crayon-tools" style="font-size: 12px !important;height: 18px !important; line-height: 18px !important;"><div class="crayon-button crayon-nums-button" title="Toggle Line Numbers"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div><div class="crayon-button crayon-plain-button" title="Toggle Plain Code"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div><div class="crayon-button crayon-wrap-button" title="Toggle Line Wrap"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div><div class="crayon-button crayon-expand-button" title="Expand Code"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div><div class="crayon-button crayon-copy-button" title="Copy"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div><div class="crayon-button crayon-popup-button" title="Open Code In New Window"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div></div></div>
			<div class="crayon-info" style="min-height: 16.8px !important; line-height: 16.8px !important;"></div>
			<div class="crayon-plain-wrap"><textarea  class="crayon-plain print-no" data-settings="dblclick" readonly style="-moz-tab-size:4; -o-tab-size:4; -webkit-tab-size:4; tab-size:4; font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important;">
from PIL import Image
im = Image.open("/Users/pickleRICK/19.png")

def imparse(a):
	width, height = im.size
	counter = 0
	b = [0] * (width * height * 3)
	for y in range(height - 1, -1, -1):
		for x in range(0,width):
			pixel = im.getpixel((x,y))
			b[counter * 3 + 2] = pixel[0] # R
			b[counter * 3 + 1] = pixel[1] # G
			b[counter * 3 + 0] = pixel[2] # B
			counter += 1
	while b[-1] == 0:
		del b[-1]
	return b</textarea></div>

<p>After it returns, the byte array gets passed to the now familiar decode function and then the deflate function.</p><!-- Crayon Syntax Highlighter v_2.7.2_beta -->

<!-- [Format Time: 0.0002 seconds] -->
<p>As you can see, we have the MZ header and the next binary.</p>
<p>Within the DLL are additional functions which handle executing the new payload and I’ve gone ahead and decoded some of the native API’s they use to show how they carry out activity.<br />
<img  class="alignnone size-full wp-image-43296 lozad"  data-src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/AgentTesla21.png" alt="AgentTesla21" width="974" height="76" data-srcset="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/AgentTesla21.png 974w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/AgentTesla21-900x70.png 900w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/AgentTesla21-300x23.png 300w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/AgentTesla21-768x60.png 768w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/AgentTesla21-370x29.png 370w" sizes="(max-width: 974px) 100vw, 974px" /></p>
<h2>The final payload</h2>
<p>Arrival of the last binary – another .NET application called “RII9DKFR5LC4Y669MLOA2C50SFLPHZBN61CZ160Z.exe”. If you read any of the posts mentioned earlier on the analysis of AgentTesla, then this will look familiar.</p>
<p><img  class="alignnone size-full wp-image-43176 lozad"  data-src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_16.png" alt="agenttesla_16" width="774" height="1350" data-srcset="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_16.png 774w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_16-172x300.png 172w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_16-768x1340.png 768w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_16-587x1024.png 587w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_16-370x645.png 370w" sizes="(max-width: 774px) 100vw, 774px" /></p>
<p>Function and variable names are encoded with Unicode values in the range of 0x200B-0x200E. Strings are decrypted by, in this sample, function “KMBHFDXSELJYYLVK\u3002”.</p>
<p><img  class="alignnone size-full wp-image-43200 lozad"  data-src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_17.png" alt="agenttesla_17" width="974" height="510" data-srcset="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_17.png 974w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_17-900x471.png 900w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_17-300x157.png 300w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_17-768x402.png 768w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_17-370x194.png 370w" sizes="(max-width: 974px) 100vw, 974px" /></p>
<p>This function uses a hardcoded password and salt to derive a key from the SHA1 hashing algorithm as implemented by Microsoft (modified PBKDF1). Afterwards, it uses the key and hardcoded IV to decrypt the string with AES-CBC.</p>
<p><img  class="alignnone size-full wp-image-43224 lozad"  data-src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_18.png" alt="agenttesla_18" width="975" height="488" data-srcset="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_18.png 975w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_18-900x450.png 900w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_18-300x150.png 300w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_18-768x384.png 768w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_18-370x185.png 370w" sizes="(max-width: 975px) 100vw, 975px" /></p>
<p>A quick Google for that IV shows hundreds of results for it, with most revolving around an encryption example that was used as the base for this function – it even copies the examples variable names.</p>
<p>What I found interesting here is that none of these values ever change sample to sample. Even going back to the samples in the write-ups on AgentTesla from over 6 months ago, I was able to decrypt their base64 strings listed in the blog. This confirms the same values are in use and likely hard coded into the builder for AgentTesla.</p>
<p>Given that everything is static then, it’s fairly trivial to extract all of the base64 encoded strings, decrypt them, and look for interesting IoC’s.</p><!-- Crayon Syntax Highlighter v_2.7.2_beta -->

		<div id="crayon-68c8c6028fa00494480713" class="crayon-syntax crayon-theme-classic crayon-font-monaco crayon-os-pc print-yes notranslate" data-settings=" minimize scroll-mouseover wrap" style=" margin-top: 12px; margin-bottom: 12px; font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important;">
		
			<div class="crayon-toolbar" data-settings=" mouseover overlay hide delay" style="font-size: 12px !important;height: 18px !important; line-height: 18px !important;"><span class="crayon-title"></span>
			<div class="crayon-tools" style="font-size: 12px !important;height: 18px !important; line-height: 18px !important;"><div class="crayon-button crayon-nums-button" title="Toggle Line Numbers"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div><div class="crayon-button crayon-plain-button" title="Toggle Plain Code"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div><div class="crayon-button crayon-wrap-button" title="Toggle Line Wrap"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div><div class="crayon-button crayon-expand-button" title="Expand Code"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div><div class="crayon-button crayon-copy-button" title="Copy"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div><div class="crayon-button crayon-popup-button" title="Open Code In New Window"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div></div></div>
			<div class="crayon-info" style="min-height: 16.8px !important; line-height: 16.8px !important;"></div>
			<div class="crayon-plain-wrap"><textarea  class="crayon-plain print-no" data-settings="dblclick" readonly style="-moz-tab-size:4; -o-tab-size:4; -webkit-tab-size:4; tab-size:4; font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important;">
from Crypto.Cipher import AES
def stringdecrypt(a):
	string = base64.b64decode(a)
	iv = "@1B2c3D4e5F6g7H8"
	key = "\x34\x88\x6D\x5B\x09\x7A\x94\x19\x78\xD0\xE3\x8b\x1b\x5c\xa3\x29\x60\x74\x6a\x5e\x5d\x64\x87\x11\xb1\x2c\x67\xaa\x5b\x3a\x8e\xbf" #to 6a/5e for first iteration
	cleartext = AES.new(key[0:32], AES.MODE_CBC, iv).decrypt(string)
	return cleartext

fh = open("extractedb64")
content = fh.readlines()
fh.close()
for i in content:
	try:
		dec = stringdecrypt(i)
		print "%s | %s" % (i.strip(),dec.strip())
	except:
		pass</textarea></div>
			<div class="crayon-main" style="">
				<table class="crayon-table">

<!-- [Format Time: 0.0004 seconds] -->
<p>File names, registry keys, and e-mails to start off hunting with. You can also see where the corresponding base64 is within the code and then use dnSpy to obtain further context on how AgentTesla utilizes these values.</p>

<p>Pivoting to these values in dnSpy will land you in a function that seems responsible for sending the stolen data back to the attacker.<br />
<img  class="alignnone size-full wp-image-43248 lozad"  data-src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_19.png" alt="agenttesla_19" width="974" height="442" data-srcset="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_19.png 974w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_19-900x408.png 900w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_19-300x136.png 300w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_19-768x349.png 768w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_19-370x168.png 370w" sizes="(max-width: 974px) 100vw, 974px" /></p>
<h2>Pivoting on a hunch</h2>
<p>At this point, I’ve accomplished the goal I set out for – covering the packing techniques used by the current version of AgentTesla, offering some code to automate unpacking and decrypt some configuration data.</p>
<p>But why stop when you’re ahead?</p>
<p>I like to Google static values and constants when analyzing malware because you can usually find some interesting stuff – configurations, forums, accounts, panels, etc. When I began searching for the file “one.jpeg.png.exe” I stumbled across a site, “b-f-v[.]info”, which hosts various versions of this keylogger.</p>
<p><img  class="alignnone size-full wp-image-43272 lozad"  data-src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_20.png" alt="agenttesla_20" width="975" height="282" data-srcset="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_20.png 975w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_20-900x260.png 900w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_20-300x87.png 300w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_20-768x222.png 768w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/agenttesla_20-370x107.png 370w" sizes="(max-width: 975px) 100vw, 975px" /></p>
<p>They all function in the same way but the image that displays is related to the first part of the file name. The images are various sizes so the decoding would be different for each; however, the code shown previously will grab the correct Width and Height for building the array.</p>
<p>Also take note of the dates and when they were modified. The sample covered in this blog was seen on August 29<sup>th</sup>, just two days before these were created – so the person or group behind these appear to be actively creating new versions to send out. I confirmed in these samples we find the same SMTP credentials.</p>
<h2>Conclusion</h2>
<p>Hopefully this overview of their packing techniques, along with the scripts to unpack each phase, prove helpful to others when looking at AgentTesla. Given its recent spikes in popularity, it’s likely not going anywhere anytime soon so the more knowledge you have of the threat, the better you can defend yourself.</p>
<p>You can continue to track this threat through the <a href="https://autofocus.paloaltonetworks.com/">Palo Alto Networks AutoFocus AgentTesla tag</a> and you will find the hashes for all of the files covered in this blog below:</p>

<p>Initial PE32</p>

<p>mypic.jpeg.png.exe | cb0de059cbd5eba8c61c67bedcfa399709e40246039a0457ca6d92697ea516f9</p>
<p>familyhome.jpeg.png.exe / myhome.jpeg.png.exe | 444e9fbf683e2cff9f1c64808d2e6769c13ed6b29899060d7662d1fe56c3121b</p>
<p>gift-certificate.pdf.png.exe | 124bb13ede19e56927fe5afc5baf680522586534727babbe1aa1791d116caeeb</p>

<script>

(function($) {
	// Migrated from the unit42-v5 + Modifications
	var subscribeSuccess = false;
	var email = document.getElementById('newsletter-email');
	var subscription_form = document.getElementById('unit42footerSubscription_form');
	var subscription_form_button = document.getElementById('unit42footerSubscription_form_button');
	window.captchaComplete = function() {
		subscribeSuccess = true;
		if ($(mail).val() != '' && isEmail($(mail).val())) {
			$(subscription_form_button).removeClass('is-disabled');
		}
		setTimeout(function() {
			$(email).focus();
			$('.g-recaptcha iframe').attr('tabindex', '-1');
		}, 100)
	}
	window.captchaExpires = function() {
		subscribeSuccess = false;
		$(subscription_form_button).addClass('is-disabled', true);
	} 
	$(subscription_form).submit(function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		e.stopImmediatePropagation();
		updateEmailMask();
		var success = true;
		var form = $(this);
		var mail = form.find('input[name="Email"]');
		
		if (mail.val() === '') {
			mail.addClass('has-error');
			showError(1);
			success = false;	
		}  else if (!isEmail(mail.val())){
			showError(2);
			success = false;
		}
		else {
			mail.removeClass('has-error');
			$('.error-mail').addClass('d-none');
		}

		if (!subscribeSuccess) {
			$('.error-recaptcha').removeClass('d-none');
		} else {
			$('.error-recaptcha').addClass('d-none');
		}

		if (success && subscribeSuccess) {
			$.ajax({
				type: 'POST',
				url: form.attr('action'),
				data: form.serialize(),
				beforeSend: function() {
                 form.find('button').addClass('is-loading');
    			},
				success: function(msg) {
					form.find('.form-success-message').html('<p class="success-message">You have been successfully subscribed</p>');
					form.find('button').removeClass('is-loading');
					$(email).val('');
					clearError();
				},
				error: function(jqXHR, textStatus, errorThrown) {
					$(subscription_form_button).addClass('is-disabled', true);
					form.find('button').removeClass('is-loading');
					
				}
			});
		}
		return false;
	});
	function showError(error_type){
		if(error_type == 1) {
			$('.error-mail').text("Please enter the email address.").addClass('error-show');
			$(subscription_form_button).addClass('is-disabled');
		} else if(error_type == 2){
			$('.error-mail').text("Please provide a valid e-mail address.").addClass('error-show');
			$(subscription_form_button).addClass('is-disabled');
		}	
		$(subscription_form_button).removeClass('is-loading');
	}
	function clearError(){
		$('.error-mail').text("").removeClass('error-show');;
		$(subscription_form_button).removeClass('is-loading');
		$(subscription_form_button).removeClass('is-disabled');
	}
	
	$(email).on('input', function (event) { 
		var email = $(this).val();
		if (isEmail(email) ) {			
			clearError();
		} else if(email == ""){
			clearError();
		} else{
			showError(2);			  
		}
    });

	function isEmail(email) {
		var re = /^(([^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+(\.[^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+)*)|(".+"))@((\[[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\])|(([a-zA-Z\-0-9]+\.)+[a-zA-Z]{2,}))$/;
		return re.test(String(email).toLowerCase());
	}
	
	var captcha_loaded = false;
         if(!captcha_loaded){
            // recaptcha on foucs call
            $(document).on('change paste keyup', '#newsletter-email', function () {
               if($('.g-recaptcha').hasClass('d-none')){
                $('.g-recaptcha').removeClass('d-none');
               }
               if(!captcha_loaded ){
                  captcha_loaded  = true;
                  // trigger loading api.js (recaptcha.js) script
                  var head = document.getElementsByTagName('head')[0];
                  var script = document.createElement('script');
                  script.type = 'text/javascript';
                  script.src = 'https://www.google.com/recaptcha/api.js?hl=en_US';
                  head.appendChild(script);
               }
            });
        }
	
	function updateEmailMask() {
		var email = $("#unit42footerSubscription_form input[name='Email']").val();
		if (email && email.trim() != '') {
			var maskedEmail = maskEmailAddress(email);
			$("#unit42footerSubscription_form input[name='emailFormMask']").val(maskedEmail);
		}
	}

	function maskEmailAddress (emailAddress) {
		function mask(str) {
			var strLen = str.length;
			if (strLen > 4) {
				return str.substr(0, 1) + str.substr(1, strLen - 1).replace(/\w/g, '*') + str.substr(-1,1);
			}
			return str.replace(/\w/g, '*');
		}
		return emailAddress.replace(/([\w.]+)@([\w.]+)(\.[\w.]+)/g, function (m, p1, p2, p3) {
			return mask(p1) + '@' + mask(p2) + p3;
		});

		return emailAddress;
	}

}(jQuery));
//# sourceMappingURL=main.js.map
	
</script>

<script type="text/javascript">
	$(document).ready(function(){
		$("html").on("click", ".language-btn", function () {
			$(this).toggleClass("expand");
			$(".language-dropdown").toggleClass("expand");
		});

		$("html").on("click", "body", function (b) {
			if (!$(b.target).is(".language-btn")) {
				$(".language-btn").removeClass("expand");
				$(".language-dropdown").removeClass("expand");
			}
		});
	});
</script>

	var isProcessing = false; 
    function alter_ul_post_values(obj,post_id,ul_type){
	
		if (isProcessing)    
		return;  
		isProcessing = true;   
		var like_nonce = jQuery('#_wpnonce').val();
		jQuery(obj).find("span").html("..");
                jQuery.ajax({
                    type: "POST",
                    url: "https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/plugins/like-dislike-counter-for-posts-pages-and-comments/ajax_counter.php",
                    data: "post_id="+post_id+"&up_type="+ul_type+"&ul_nonce="+like_nonce,
                    success: function(msg){
                            jQuery(obj).find("span").html(msg);
                            isProcessing = false; 
                            jQuery(obj).find('svg').children('path').attr('stroke','#0050FF');
                            jQuery(obj).removeClass('idc_ul_cont_not_liked idc_ul_cont_not_liked_inner');
                    }
 		});
	}

    event.preventDefault();

       OneTrust.ToggleInfoDisplay();
    }else{

}

function loadScript(url, defer){
    var script1 = document.createElement('script');
    script1.setAttribute('type', 'text/javascript');
    script1.setAttribute('src',url);
    if(defer == true){
        script1.setAttribute('defer','defer');
    }
    document.head.appendChild(script1);
}
function loadScript1(url, callback){

    var script = document.createElement("script")
    script.type = "text/javascript";
    if (script.readyState){  //IE
        script.onreadystatechange = function(){
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" || script.readyState == "complete"){
                script.onreadystatechange = null;
                callback();
            }
        };
    } else {  //Others
        script.onload = function(){
            callback();
        };
    }
    script.src = url;
    document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
}
if(referer == "CloudCortex" || referer == "Prisma" || referer == "Cortex" || referer == "Sase" || referer == "Unit" || referer == "Ngfw"){

    setTimeout(function(){
      loadScript(main_site_criticalTopBase, false);
      loadScript1(main_site_criticalTopProductNav, function(){

      });

        window.PAN_initializeProduct2021Nav();
      });
      loadScript(main_site_defered, false);
    }, 3000);
	}
}

document.addEventListener("DOMContentLoaded", function() {
  // Find all the forms on the page
  var forms = document.getElementsByTagName("form");

  // Loop through each form
  for (var i = 0; i < forms.length; i++) {
    var form = forms[i];

    // Check if the hidden input field already exists
    var requestInput = form.querySelector("input[name='web_unit42_req_id']");
    if (!requestInput) {
      // Create a hidden input element if it doesn't exist
      requestInput = document.createElement("input");
      requestInput.type = "hidden";
      requestInput.name = "web_unit42_req_id";
      requestInput.id = "web_unit42_req_id";

      // Append the hidden field to the form
      form.appendChild(requestInput);
    }
  }
});

// Assign the onCheqResponse function to the window object to handle events from any form
window.onCheqResponse = function(encryptedMessage, requestID) {
  // Find all the forms on the page
  var forms = document.getElementsByTagName("form");

  for (var i = 0; i < forms.length; i++) {
    var form = forms[i];
    var requestInput = form.querySelector("input[name='web_unit42_req_id']");
    if (requestInput) {
      requestInput.value = requestID;
    }
  }
};
